This is Organic Chemistry Chapter one: Nomenclature. And, if you think about it, in the late eighteen hundred's, organic chemistry was, well, it was essentially the Wild West. Yeah, totally. Yeah. Welcome everyone to the organic chemistry chapter one nomenclature MCAT lecture. But yeah, you had like brilliant scientists isolating life saving compounds in London and then other scientists synthesizing the exact same molecules in say Paris or Berlin. But progress was constantly stalling. It was stalling because they were just naming these molecules based on whatever arbitrary features they felt like at the time. Literally whatever they wanted. Like one person would name a compound after the plant it was extracted from. Yeah, or another would name it based on the fact that it smelled like rancid butter or something. Exactly. Or like the color it turned when you lit it on fire. It was absolute chaos. I mean, was no way to read a paper and actually know if you were working on the same molecule as the chemist across the ocean. Right, which is a complete bottleneck for scientific advancement. You can't build a global collaborative science if you don't share a universal language. And that chaos is exactly why a governing body had to step in, right? They had to lay down an absolute, uncompromising set of laws. The International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry, IUPAC. Right, IUPAC. They created this system to ensure like an unambiguous one to one relationship between a chemical name and its three d physical structure. And that's why we are starting right here today at The Absolute Foundation, because of a pretty harsh reality about test day for you. Oh absolutely. But nomenclature is the grammar of physical reality. If you don't know the grammar, can't read the sentence. That's why today, we're going to be incredibly methodical. We're gonna build this language from the ground up. Exactly. We will define every single term, every structure, and every mechanism before we even use it. So let's start with the alphabet, basically. Section 1.1, IUPAC naming conventions, the famous five steps. Yeah, let's actually put them to work instead of just listing them. Step one is identify the longest carbon chain, the parent chain. I always think of this as pouring the concrete slab foundation for a house. That's a great way to look at it. Everything else, you know, the garage, the bay window, the porch, is just a substituent built onto that main structure. Right. The parent chain dictates the root of the entire chemical name. But, the first trap students fall into here is looking at a two d drawing on the screen and just counting the straightest, most horizontal line of carbons they see. Oh yeah. Molecules are not rigid, straight lines. They bend, they zigzag, they twist all over the place. Exactly. You have to physically trace every possible continuous path of carbons to ensure you actually have the longest one. Because the test makers will deliberately draw the longest chain snaking around a corner and going up to the top of the molecule just to see if you're actually paying attention. They absolutely will. But wait, let me throw a complication at this. What if I'm looking at like a massive molecule? I trace a continuous chain of 10 carbons. Like bog at BK. But somewhere else in the molecule branching off is a shorter chain of six carbons a hexene. But that six carbon chain contains a highly oxidized functional group, like a carboxylic acid. Who gets to be the foundation? Oh, the functional group takes ultimate precedence every Right. Yeah. The rule is that the parent chain absolutely must contain the highest priority functional group in the molecule. Even if there's a longer chain right next to it. Even if there is a longer physical chain of carbons sitting right there, you ignore it. If that six carbon chain holds the highest priority group, your root name is based on hexane. Wow. Okay. So the 10 carbon chain just gets demoted. Yep. It gets demoted to a mere substituent hanging off the side. The functional group is the VIP. It has to be on the main stage. Okay. Makes sense. Let's complicate it further though. Molecule. I find the VIP the highest priority functional group. I trace out from it, and I find two different paths that both give me a chain of eight carbons. An octane. Okay, a tie. Right. They both contain the VIP group. They're identical in length. How do I break that tie to choose the true parent chain? Well the tiebreaker for identical length chains is substitution. Substitution, meaning what exactly? You look at both of those potential eight carbon chains and count how many branches are attached directly to them. The chain that is more substituted so, the one with the greater number of distinct branches hanging off it, wins the title of parent chain. Why does IUPAC do that? Why prefer the more substituted chain? It actually simplifies the naming of the substituents later on. It's a bookkeeping trick. If you choose the less substituted chain as your parent, you end up having to name incredibly complex, highly branched substituents, which requires like messy nested parentheses in the final name. Oh, that sounds awful. It is. By choosing the chain with the most individual simple branches, the final name is just much cleaner. Okay, we poured our concrete slab. We found the longest chain containing the highest priority group. And we broke ties by choosing maximum substitution. Step two: number the chain. Right. We have to assign a numerical value starting from one to every carbon atom in that parent chain. And you only have two choices, right? You start at one end or you start at the other? Exactly. And the strict rule here is that numbering must start at the end that is closest to the highest priority functional group. Because the goal is to give that VIP functional group the lowest possible number. Precisely. The VIP needs to be in the front row. But we keep saying highest priority. How do you actually determine priority? Is there just like a master list I have to brute force memorize? Well, you will eventually memorize the order, but it is entirely based on a physical property: oxidation state. In organic chemistry nomenclature, priority is overwhelmingly determined by how oxidized a carbon atom is. The more oxidized the carbon, the higher its priority. Let's define oxidized for a pre med audience. We aren't necessarily talking about a literal loss of an electron to form an ion like you'd see in a general chemistry galvanic cell. Right, no, we're talking about bonds. Oxidation state increases when a carbon atom forms more bonds to heteroatoms like oxygen, nitrogen or halogens and fewer bonds to hydrogen. Okay, so a carbon with three bonds to oxygen, like in a carboxylic acid, is highly oxidized. Exactly. Yeah. And it will vastly outrank a carbon with only one bond to oxygen, like in alcohol. Makes sense. So you scan the molecule, find the most oxidized group, and you start numbering from the end of the chain that gets you to that group the fastest. You got it. Let's talk about tiebreakers again, though. What if the molecule is perfectly symmetrical? Say it's an eight carbon chain with the exact same functional group at carbon two and carbon seven. In that case, if the main functional groups are tied, you look at the other substituents, the alkyl branches or the halogens. You number the chain in whichever direction gives the lowest possible numbers or locants to those remaining substituents. Got it. And what if the main structure isn't a straight chain at all? What if it's a ring, like a cyclooccane? The logic remains exactly the same. Since a ring doesn't have a distinct left or right end, you just start numbering at the carbon that holds the highest priority functional group. So that carbon is automatically assigned number one. Right. Then you proceed around the ring, either clockwise or counterclockwise, in whichever direction gives the lowest possible numbers to the other substituents. Okay, there is one edge case here that I know the MCAT just loves to test. Oh, I bet I know which one. Let's say I have a molecule that contains both a double bond and a triple bond. I trace the parent chain. Numbering from the left gives the double bond position two. Numbering from the right gives the triple bond position two. It's a dead tie for the lowest number. Who wins? Ah, yeah. If you are forced to choose in a tie scenario, double bonds actually take precedence over triple bonds in numbering. Wait, really? Yeah, you assign the lower number to the double bond. It feels somewhat counterintuitive because the triple bond has more pi electrons, but the IUPAC convention favors the 'n' over the 'n' for numbering priority. Okay, good to know. So we've poured the foundation, we've numbered the carbon atoms from end to end. Step three: Name the substituents. These are the additions built onto our main structure. Right. A substituent is any functional group or carbon chain that is not part of the parent chain. For standard carbon chain substituents, the alkyl groups, we name them just like normal alkyns, but we drop the -nn suffix and replace it with -chau. So methane becomes a methyl group, ethane becomes an ethyl group. Exactly. But once we get to three or four carbons, the actual shape of the substituent really matters, right? Three carbons isn't always just a straight line sticking out. No, it's not. And that introduces the prefixes. You will frequently see N propyl. The N stands for normal. It indicates a straight unbranched chain of carbons. Okay. But what if that three carbon chain is attached to the parent chain by its middle carbon? Like it forms a Y shape? That is the isopropyl group. Iso generally refers to a structure that splits into two equal methyl groups at the end, kind of like a snake's tongue. I like that visual. Then we get to the four carbon substituents, the butyl groups. Right, you have N butyl, which is straight, you have sec butyl where the attachment point is on the secondary carbon of the chain, and you have tert butyl or T butyl. Tert butyl looks like a little chicken foot to me. It's a central carbon attached to the parent chain and that central carbon is surrounded by three methyl groups. It is incredibly bulky. Very bulky. In fact, knowing how bulky tert butyl is will save you on stereochemistry questions later because that steric hindrance heavily dictates how reactions proceed. Absolutely. So we have our substituent names. Step four: Assign numbers. We just pair each named substituent with its corresponding numerical Seems simple, right? But there is a massive trap here. Let's say I'm numbering my parent chain and I notice that carbon number three has a methyl group attached to it. But wait, carbon number three actually has two methyl groups attached to it, one pointing up, one pointing down. Okay, geminal methyls. And carbon number five also has a methyl group. So I have three methyl groups total. I know I have to use the prefix tri, so it's a trimethyl compound. Do I just say three thoful five trimethyl? Oh I see where this is going. If you say three thors and five trimethyl you will build the wrong three d model. Exactly. Every single substituent gets its own distinct number even if they are attached to the exact same carbon atom. So if you have three methyl groups you absolutely must provide three numbers? Yes. The correct designation is three tau tern three found in five trimethyl. The commas separate the numbers. The fact that three is repeated proves that both of those distinct methyl groups are tethered to the exact same spot on the concrete slab. Group. It's ambiguous and IUPAC hates ambiguity. Hates it. Which brings us to step five: complete the name. This is where we stitch all the pieces together. The rule is that we must alphabetize the substituents before attaching them to front of the parent chain name. Alphabetizing. It sounds like kindergarten, but I know this is where the test makers lay down their highest yield traps. Oh for sure. Because we aren't just looking at the first letter of whatever's written down. You have to actively ignore certain prefixes when alphabetizing. Which ones? You ignore the quantity prefixes, di, tri, tetra, and you ignore hyphenated prefixes, sectert, en. So if I have a dimethyl group and an ethyl group, I just ignore the D, I'm comparing the M in methyl to the E in ethyl. Exactly. E comes first in the alphabet. So the ethyl group is written before the dimethyl group. But there's a caveat here, isn't there? One that separates the prepared students from those who are just guessing. There is. You do include non hyphenated modifiers when alphabetizing. Prefixes like iso, neo, and cyclo are considered intrinsic parts of the fundamental name. Wait, so an isopropyl group is alphabetized by the I, not the P? Yes, exactly. Compare tert butyl to isobutyl. For tert butyl, you ignore the tert and alphabetized by the B. For isobutyl, you include the iso and alphabetize by the I. It's incredibly specific. It is, but it's the kind of specific rule that the MCAT uses to test your attention to detail. Totally. Let's pull this all together. We have alphabetized our substituents. We've placed them in front of our parent chain, and the parent chain ends with the suffix of our highest priority functional group. We have our two d skeleton. Right. But, you know, molecules are not flat drawings on paper. They exist in three d space. And a chemical name isn't truly complete unless it defines the exact spatial orientation of its atoms. We have to add stereochemical labels. Exactly. This is where we take our two d schematic and build the three d model. Let's start with chiral centers. A carbon atom attached to four distinct different groups. The spatial arrangement of those four groups matters immensely, especially in biology, because receptors in the human body are stereo specific. One arrangement might cure a headache and the mirror image arrangement might be completely inactive. Or even toxic. Yeah, or toxic. To define that arrangement, we use the RNS system, which is governed by the Conning Gold Pre Log Priority Rules. Let's walk through those rules because it's not based on oxidation state anymore, is it? No, not at all. For stereochemistry, priority is strictly assigned by atomic number. You look at the four atoms directly attached to the chiral center. The higher the atomic number, the higher the priority. Okay, so an oxygen atom, atomic number eight, will always outrank a nitrogen atom, atomic number seven. Yep. And nitrogen outranks carbon, which is six. And carbon always outranks hydrogen, which is one. So hydrogen is almost always priority number four, the lowest priority. Exactly. But what happens when there is a tie? What if your chiral center is attached to a methyl group and an ethyl group? Both attached atoms are carbons. You can't just stop at the first atom, right? You have to trace the path outward. You do. You look at what those two tied carbons are attached to. The methyl carbon is attached to three hydrogens. The ethyl carbon is attached to two hydrogens and one carbon. You compare the lists of attached atoms in decreasing order of atomic number. The ethyl carbons list is carbon, hydrogen, hydrogen. The methyl carbons list is hydrogen, hydrogen, hydrogen. Carbon beats hydrogen at the first point of difference. So the ethyl group wins priority. Ah, you just keep walking outward one shell at a time until the tie breaks. And I remember double bonds count as two single bonds to that atom. Right. Okay, so we've ranked our four groups from one to four. Now we have to assign R or S. This requires some mental gymnastics. It does. You must mentally orient the molecule in three d space so that the lowest priority group number four, usually hydrogen, is pointing away from you into the back of the page on a dashed line. I always picture grabbing the steering wheel of a car. The steering column, the piece going down into the dashboard away from you, is that lowest priority group. Then you look at the other three groups arranged on the steering wheel itself facing you. That's a perfect visualization. You draw a curve from priority one to priority two to priority three. If that curve flows clockwise, like turning the steering wheel to the right, the configuration is R. R stands for rectus, Latin for right. And if the curve flows counterclockwise, turning the wheel to the left, the configuration is S. S stands for sinister, Latin for left. Correct. But the MCAT isn't always nice enough to draw the molecule with the hydrogen pointing to the back. What if the lowest priority group is on a wedge pointing right at your face? Well, have two options. You can mentally rotate the entire molecule 180 degrees, which honestly is prone to error under time pressure. Yeah, I wouldn't recommend that. Or you can just use the shortcut. You draw your circle one to two to three exactly as it is drawn on the page. If it looks R, but the hydrogen is pointing at you, you just flip the result. It is actually S. And the reverse is true. If it looks S, it is actually R. The flip trick is a lifesaver. It really is. Okay, that covers chiral centers. What about double bonds? We can't have free rotation around the pi bond, so the groups attached are locked in place. For alkenes we use the E and Z system. Yes, and you use the exact same conning goal pre log rules based on atomic number. But you apply them differently. You mentally split the double bond in half vertically. So you look at the left carbon of the double bond, and you look at the groups attached to it. You assign priority one and two just to that left side. Right. Then you go to the right carbon of the double bond, and you assign priority one and two to its groups. Then what? Then you look at the entire double bond horizontally. Where are the two number one priority groups located relative to each other? If both highest priority groups are on the same side of the double bond, either both pointing up or both pointing down, the double bond gets the label Zee. The classic mnemonic that everyone uses because it just works. Zee stands for the German word zussamen meaning together, but we all just remember it as zeimside. Zee is for the same side. Exactly. And if the two highest priority groups are on opposite sides of the double bond, one pointing up, one pointing down, it gets the label E for in giggen, which translates to opposite. Okay, so we have our EZ RNS labels, we know the three d shape. Where do these letters physically go in the written IUPIC name? Do we just sprinkle them next to the substituents? No. They are placed in parentheses at the very front of the completely alphabetized IUPIC name. They are the preface to the molecule. Oh, I see. For example, if carbon two is an archairal center and a double bond starting at carbon three is e, the entire name begins strictly with 2R3E followed by a hyphen and then the rest of the chemical name. That is the complete grammar. We have the foundation, the branches, the alphabetization, and the three d spatial orientation. Now we are going to start building our vocabulary. We are moving to section 1.2, hydrocarbons and alcohols. Yeah, let's do it. We're gonna use the oxidation ladder as our narrative through line today. We are starting at the absolute bottom, Level zero, and we are going to slowly climb our way up to the most complex molecules on the exam. Level zero consists entirely of hydrocarbons. As the name implies, these are molecules composed exclusively of carbon and hydrogen. No halogens. Exactly. Because there are no bonds to electronegative heteroatoms, these carbons are at the lowest possible oxidation state. They are the least oxidized organic molecules. The simplest of these are the alkanes. These consist only of single bonds. Every carbon is sp3 hybridized, forming tetrahedral geometries. The general chemical formula is CNH2N plus two. So if you have a four carbon chain, you have two times four plus two, which is 10 hydrogens. We discussed the prefixes earlier, but they must be flawlessly, right? Oh absolutely. Meth for one, eth for two, prop for three, but for four. Methane, ethane, propane, butane. And pass four we just use standard Greek roots. Pentane for five, hexane for six, heptane for seven, octane for eight, nonen for nine, decane for 10. As you probably know, undecane for 11 and dodecane for 12, just in case. Fair enough. Alkanes are the raw carbon skeleton. They are relatively unreactive because carbon carbon and carbon hydrogen single bonds are strong and non polar. But if we remove two hydrogens and introduce a degree of unsaturation, we create multiple bonds. Alkanes and alkanes. An alkene contains at least one carbon carbon double bond That changes the hybridization of those carbons to sensp2, making them trigonal planar. The suffix changes to exnan. And alkene contains at least one carbon carbon triple bond. Those carbons are spot hybridized, making them completely linear. The suffix changes to same. And returning to our grammar rules, the location of that multiple bond must be stated. The convention is that the lower numbered carbon involved in the bond dictates the position. If a double bond bridges carbon two and carbon three of a pentane chain, the molecule is two pentan. Though in more modern IUPAC formatting, which the MCAT uses frequently, that number is wedged right before the suffix. So, pent 2n. It specifically locks the number two to the n n double bond. Right, both formats are valid. Now let's climb the ladder. We take our level zero hydrocarbon, we remove one of those hydrogen atoms, and we replace it with a hydroxyl group and an Oh group. We have officially taken one step up the oxidation ladder. We reach level one, the alcohols. The introduction of that hydroxyl group completely fundamentally alters the physical properties of the molecule. We go from a non polar alkane to something entirely different. Why is that? It comes down to electronegativity and hybridization. The oxygen atom in an alcohol is P3 hybridized. It has two single bonds and two lone pairs of electrons. Oxygen is significantly more electronegative than the carbon and the hydrogen it is attached to. It acts as an electron hog. So it pulls the shared electrons in those covalent bonds toward itself. Exactly. This unequal sharing creates a massive permanent dipole moment. The oxygen holds a partial negative charge and the hydrogen attached to it is stripped of electron density, holding a strong partial positive charge. And this extreme polarity unlocks a specific type of intermolecular force: hydrogen bonding. Which means alcohol molecules act like incredibly strong tiny magnets sticking to each other. The partial positive hydrogen of one molecule is highly attracted to the partial negative oxygen lone pairs of a neighboring molecule. Because they stick together so tightly, it takes significantly more heat energy to pull them apart and boil them into a gas compared to a simple non polar alkane of similar size. That is the macroscopic consequence of the microscopic structure. Alcohols have very high boiling points. Now for nomenclature, if the alcohol is the highest priority functional group on the molecule, it takes over the parent chain name and changes the suffix to -ol. Ethane becomes ethanol, propidane becomes propanol. But if it's outranked, say we have a molecule with both an alcohol and a carboxylic acid, the alcohol loses its VIP status. It gets demoted to a mere substituent and we use the prefix hydroxy- like three hydroxybutynoic acid. While IUPAC is the standard, the MPAD expects you to instantly recognize the common names for the simplest alcohols, right? Yeah, ethyl alcohol is simply the common name for ethanol. Isopropyl alcohol is the common name for two propanol. What about molecules that have two hydroxyl groups? Those are called diols, or sometimes glycols. Because they have two hydroxyl groups, their capacity for hydrogen bonding is doubled, pushing their boiling points even higher. There's a naming convention here that always requires a good mnemonic. There are two main structural flavors of diols: Geminal diols and Viscinal diols. To keep them straight, I just use astrology. Oh, I like that. Yeah, Geminal comes from Gemini, the twins. Geminal diols are like twins. The two hydroxyl groups are paired on the same carbon atom. Viscinodiols, on the other hand, are in the vicinity of each other. The hydroxyl groups are neighbors they are on adjacent carbon atoms. That perfectly distinguishes them. Now, let's apply some chemical logic here. You will frequently see vicinodiols in biological pathways and chemistry problems. Ethylene glycol is a very common vicinaldiol. But you will almost never see geminal dioles isolated in a stable state on the exam. Wait, really? If I put two Oh groups on the same carbon, what happens to it? Think about the physical reality of that structure. You have a single speed three hybridized carbon. It is tetrahedral, so the bond angles are roughly 109.5 degrees. You are forcing two bulky oxygen atoms, both carrying lone pairs and partial negative charges, into very close proximity on that single carbon. Oh, the electron clouds are going to repel each other massively. It's intense steric and electronic crowding. It's a high energy unstable state. Because of this extreme repulsion, geminal diols, which are also called hydrates, tend to spontaneously dehydrate. They dehydrate? Yeah, they violently kick out a molecule of water, two hydrogens and one oxygen, to relieve that stress. When they jettison that water, the remaining oxygen forms a double bond with the carbon to satisfy its octet. They spontaneously collapse into highly stable carbonyl compounds. Is mind blowing. The molecule essentially says this is too crowded, kicks out water and transforms its entire functional group. Pretty much. We are going to talk about carbonyls next, but before we leave alcohols, we have to look at a classic MCAT priority battle. This is a very common trap. Picture a molecule that has both a carbon carbon double bond, an alkene, and an alcohol hydroxyl group on the same parent chain. The MCAT will ask you to number the chain and name the compound. So we have an alkene fighting an alcohol for the lowest number. Who wins? Returning to our universal rule. Priority is determined by oxidation state. The carbon atoms in the alkene are bonded only to other carbons and hydrogens. But the carbon attached to the hydroxyl group has one bond to a heteroatom, oxygen. So it is at a higher oxidation state. Therefore, the alcohol decisively wins the priority battle. It gets the lower number, and it provides the final suffix ol. So if you have a seven carbon chain, and you have a double bond at carbon six, and an alcohol at carbon two, the numbering must start from the side closest to the alcohol. The name would be hept six on two ol. The alkene is just a feature of the chain, but the alcohol is the boss. Exactly. Follow the oxidation state, and the rules are completely predictable. Which transitions us perfectly to Section 1.3: Aldehydes and Ketones. Let's trace the chemistry from what you just described. You said that if a geminal diol is too crowded, it spontaneously dehydrates. It loses water and forms a carbon oxygen double bond. That specific double bonded structure is our step up the ladder. We are now at level two of oxidation, the carbonyl. The carbonyl is arguably the single most important structural feature in all of organic chemistry. Structurally, it is a carbon atom double bonded to an oxygen atom. That double bond changes everything. How so? Well, the carbonyl carbon is SP2 hybridized, giving it a trigonal planar geometry. All the atoms attached to it are locked into a perfectly flat plane with bond angles of 120 degrees. The flatness is critical because it means a nucleophile can attack it from the top or the bottom without much steric hindrance. But the reactivity really comes down to the atomic tug of war happening in those bonds, right? Yes. Oxygen is significantly more electronegative than carbon. It acts as an electron sink. It constantly pulls the electrons from the pi bond toward itself. This unequal sharing leaves the oxygen atom with a substantial partial negative charge. It becomes a strong nucleophile seeking out positive charges. And the carbon loses that tug of war. Completely. The carbonyl carbon is stripped of its electron density. It is left with a strong partial positive charge. It becomes an exceptional electrophile, a prime target for attack by any passing nucleophile with extra electrons. It's an electron poor bullseye. So a carbonyl is just the C double bond O structure, but it isn't a complete functional group on its own. What is attached to the other two bonds of that flat carbon determines whether we are looking at an aldehyde or a ketone. Let's start with aldehydes. An aldehyde is defined by having the carbonyl group located at the absolute end of a carbon chain. It is a terminal functional group. The train analogy is perfect perfect here. The aldehyde is the caboose of the carbon train. It must sit at the very end of the track. Structurally, this means the carbonyl carbon is attached to the rest of the alkyl chain on one side, but on the other side it must be attached to at least one hydrogen atom. That single terminal hydrogen is the defining feature of the caboose. Because it is terminal, an aldehyde is almost always located at carbon number one. Therefore, we generally don't need to specify its number in the IUPAC name. We simply replace the e of the parent alkane with the suffix al-. Butane becomes butanol. Hexane becomes hexanol. If the aldehyde is outranked by something higher up the ladder, it gets demoted to a prefix. We use Oxo or sometimes formal. Now what if the aldehyde isn't on a chain but is attached directly to a ring like a cyclopentane? That is a specific naming exception. When attached directly to a ring we use the suffix dacarbaldihyde, so cyclopentadacarbaldihyde. But beyond IUPAC, the MCAT heavily relies on the common names for the smallest aldehydes. You just have to flashcard these. A one carbon aldehyde, methanol is formaldehyde. A two carbon aldehyde, ethanol is acetaldehyde. A three carbon aldehyde, procainal, is propionaldehyde. Formaldehyde, acetaldehyde, propionaldehyde. Knowing them instantly prevents you from freezing on a passage. So that is the terminal carbonyl. What happens if we move the carbonyl to the inside of the chain? We get a ketone. If the aldehyde is the caboose, the ketone is a passenger car. It is an internal carbonyl. It is perfectly sandwiched between two carbon chains, two alkyl groups. By definition, a ketone can never be at the end of a chain. Right. If it were at the end, it would have to be bonded to a hydrogen, making it an aldehyde. Because a keycone is internal, it can exist at multiple different positions along a parent chain. Therefore, you must specify its location with a number. We replace the E of the parent alkane with the suffix one. So a five carbon chain with the carbonyl at the second carbon is two pentanone. If it's at the third carbon, it's three pentanone. If a ketone is demoted to a substituent, we use the prefix oxo or keto. The common naming convention for ketones is very intuitive. You simply name the two alkyl groups on either side of the carbonyl alphabetically and then add the word ketone. If you have an ethyl group on one side and a methyl group on the other, it is ethylmethylketone. But there is one ketone whose common name is legendary and it defies that simple naming rule: propanone. It is the simplest possible ketone, just three carbons with the carbonyl in the middle. Everyone calls it acetone. Acetone is ubiquitous. You will see it as a solvent in organic lab passages and you will see it in biochemistry when discussing ketone bodies. Now we must address one of the most brilliant high yield troughs the MCAT uses to test if you truly understand physical chemistry. It revolves around the boiling points of aldehydes and ketones. Let me walk into the trap exactly how a student might. I am taking the test. I see a ketone. I see that massive, highly electronegative oxygen atom double bonded to the carbon. You just told me that oxygen is pulling all the electron density, creating a huge dipole. The oxygen is partially negative. Yep, that's true. So my logic is, since it has an oxygen, it must undergo hydrogen bonding exactly like an alcohol, right? So the boiling point of a ketone should be just as incredibly high as an alcohol. It sounds like perfect logic, but it contains a fatal flaw. To form a hydrogen bond with itself in a pure sample, say a beaker full of pure acetone, a molecule needs two distinct features. It needs a hydrogen bond acceptor and it needs a hydrogen bond donor. Let's define those. An acceptor is an electronegative atom with lone pairs like oxygen or nitrogen. A donor is a hydrogen atom that is physically bonded directly to an electronegative atom which strips it of its electrons and makes it capable of bridging. Exactly. Aldehydes and ketones absolutely have a hydrogen bond acceptor, the partially negative carbonyl oxygen. It's It's sitting there ready to receive a hydrogen bond. Look at the structure of a ketone. The carbonyl carbon is bonded to other carbons. Those carbons are bonded to hydrogens. Neither an aldehyde nor a ketone has a hydrogen atom bonded directly to an oxygen. Precisely. They completely lack a hydrogen bond donor. They have the receiver, but they have no transmitter. Because of this, they cannot hydrogen bond with themselves in a pure sample. Wait, so a beaker of pure acetone cannot hydrogen bond with itself? No, it cannot. It only experiences dipole interactions. It's strong, but it's not hydrogen bonding strong. Which means we can construct a definitive boiling point ranking. At the bottom you have alkenes, which only possess weak London dispersion forces. In the middle you have aldehydes and ketones, which possess stronger dipole interactions due to the carbonyl. And at the absolute top, you have alcohols, which possess true robust hydrogen bonding. Alkenes are less than carbonyls, which is less than alcohols. That is a beautifully clean, logical progression based entirely on molecular structure. Before we move on to level three, we have to plant a seed for later chapters. We talked about how the carbonyl carbon is an electrophile, a target for attack, but there's a hidden reactivity to these molecules based on that atomic tug of war. Yes. The electron withdrawing nature of the carbonyl oxygen is so incredibly strong that its inductive effect radiates down the carbon chain. Let's look at the carbon directly adjacent to the carbonyl carbon. Because the oxygen is hoarding all the electron density, the alpha carbon is feeling the pull. And in turn, the alpha carbon pulls electrons away from the hydrogens attached to it. Those carbon hydrogen bonds on the alpha carbon are severely weakened, which means those alpha hydrogens are highly acidic. A relatively mild base can come in and easily rip one of those protons off. And normally ripping a proton off a carbon to create a carbonion is extremely unfavorable. Carbon hates having a negative charge. Why is it allowed here? Because of resonance. When the base removes the alpha hydrogen, it leaves behind two electrons, creating a negative charge. But that negative charge doesn't just sit on the carbon, it delocalizes. The electrons move up, form a double bond between the alpha carbon and the carbonyl carbon, and push the pi electrons of the carbonyl up, up onto the oxygen. The negative charge is now resting comfortably on the highly electronegative oxygen atom. That resonance stabilized structure is called an enolate. And creating an enolate turns that alpha carbon into a powerful nucleophile, ready to attack other molecules. It's the foundation of complex reactions like aldol condensations. But for nomenclature in chapter one, just remember alpha hydrogens are unusually acidic. A perfect teaser. We have climbed from alkanes to alcohols to aldehydes and ketones. Now we enter section 1.4, carboxylic acids and derivatives. We are moving to level three of the oxidation ladder. Let's visualize how we get here structurally. We started with aldehyde's terminal carbonyls bonded to hydrogen and ketones, internal carbonyls bonded to carbon chains. What happens if we take that flat carbonyl structure and we attach a hydroxyl group, an LH, directly to the carbonyl carbon? You are essentially fusing a ketone and an alcohol onto the exact same carbon atom. When you do that, you reach the absolute summit of the MCAT functional group hierarchy. You create a carboxylic acid. Aside from carbon dioxide, which is fully oxidized, the carboxylic acid is the most oxidized functional group you will see on the exam. The carbon has three bonds to oxygen, a double bond to one oxygen and a single bond to the hydroxyl oxygen. Because it is so highly oxidized, it takes ultimate priority and nomenclature over almost everything else. It is the undisputed VIP. And because of its structure, it is always a terminal group. It anchors the chain, and its carbon is always assigned number one. And its defining chemical characteristic is right there in the name. It is an acid. We just spent time talking about how the alpha hydrogens of ketones are acidic, But the hydrogen attached to the hydroxyl group of a carboxylic acid is vastly more acidic. It practically jumps off the molecule. Why is it so eager to leave? It comes back to the golden rule of acidity. An acid is only as strong as the stability of its conjugate base. We have to look at what happens after the proton leaves. Okay, the hydrogen leaves as an H plus stinkation. It leaves its electron behind on the oxygen. We now have a negative charge on an oxygen atom. In an alcohol, that negative charge is stuck exactly where it is. But a carboxylic acid is special. When it loses that proton, it forms a carboxylate anion. That negative charge does not stay localized. Through resonance, the electrons can delocalize up to the carbonyl oxygen. The two oxygen atoms perfectly share the burden of that negative charge. The double bond is essentially smeared across both oxygen atoms. Because the resulting anion is so incredibly stable, the starting molecule is highly motivated to throw off the proton in the first place to achieve that stability. That makes them strong organic acids. Precisely. Now for nomenclature, we replace the e of the parent alkane with the suffix aoic acid. Butane becomes butanoic acid. Hexane becomes hexanoic acid. Simple enough, but again the common names are heavily tested. You cannot hesitate when you see these. Methanoic acid with one single carbon is formic acid. The etymology helps here, Formic acid is the primary component in ant venom. The Latin word for ant is formica. Exactly. Ethanoic acid with two carbons is acetic acid. This is the main component of vinegar. Propanoic acid with three carbons is propionic acid. Formic, acetic, propionic, know them. The MCAT also frequently tests dicarboxylic acid molecules with a carboxylic acid group anchoring both ends of the carbon chain. They act like bookends. I remember drilling these in undergrad. There are mnemonics like such good apple pie to remember the carbon length. Let's list them. A two carbon chain with an acid on both ends is oxalic acid. Three carbons is malonic acid. Four carbons is succinic acid. Five carbons is glutaric acid. Six carbons is adipic acid. And seven carbons is pomalic acid. Oxalic, malonic, succinic, lutyric, adipic, pymalic. You will see succinic and malonic acid pop up repeatedly in biochemistry metabolism passages. So the carboxylic acid is the parent molecule but it sits at the head of a very important family of We call them the carboxylic acid derivatives. They are the children of the acid, and all of these derivatives are formed through a specific overarching class of reaction called a condensation reaction. Let's conceptualize what a condensation reaction actually is on a molecular level. It is when two larger molecules collide and join together to form a single, more complex, heavier molecule. But to make the connection, a very small molecule is kicked out as a byproduct. In the case of synthesizing carboxylic acid derivatives, that small molecule being kicked out is almost universally water H2O. I use an analogy for condensation reactions that visualizes the steric reality. Imagine moving into a tiny, cramped one bedroom apartment with partner. You both bring your own furniture, but the physical volume of the space is just too small to fit both of your lives. To successfully merge into one unified household, you have to make a sacrifice. You have to throw your bulky couch out to the curb. Curb. In these reactions the two molecules are moving in together, and the water molecule is the couch being tossed out to make room. That is a highly accurate way to visualize the expulsion of a small molecule to facilitate joining. Let's look at the first class of derivatives formed by throwing out that water: esters. Structurally, we take our carboxylic acid and we replace the hydroxyl group, the Oh, with an alkoxy group, an O R group, derived from an alcohol. So instead of a carbonyl attached to an Oh, it is a carbonyl attached to an oxygen, which is then attached to another carbon chain. Nomenclature for esters is a two part process. It reads almost like a first and last name. First, you look at the esterifying alkyl group attached to the single bonded oxygen. You name that first as an adjective. Then you name the parent carboxylic acid chain, but you change the suffix to -otto. For example, if you react ethanol with acetic acid. The ethanol provides the alkyl chain, so your first word is ethyl. The acetic acid is the parent chain and it becomes ethanoate. The IUPAC name is ethylethanoate. Or using the common name for the parent acid, ethyl acetate. Ethyl acetate. And if an ester happens to react with an alcohol group on its own carbon chain, it forms a ring. We call those cyclic esters lactones. Now naming an ester is one thing, but understanding how it forms is critical. The MCAT loves the mechanism of fissure esterification. Let's not just name it, let's trace the electrons. Let's walk through the curved arrows verbally. Fissure esterification occurs when you mix a carboxylic acid and an alcohol in an acidic solution. The acidic conditions provide an abundance of free protons H plus Taos. One of these protons attaches to the carbonyl oxygen of the carboxylic acid. So the oxygen now has three bonds and takes on positive formal charge. Oxygen is highly electronegative. It despises having a positive charge. To compensate, it pulls electron density away from the carbonyl carbon even more aggressively than normal. It turns that carbonyl carbon into a hyperelectrophile. It is screaming for electrons. It is an irresistible target. Step two: Attack. The alcohol molecule floating in the solution has an oxygen atom with two lone pairs of electrons. It acts as the nucleophile. One of those lone pairs attacks the hyperelectrophilic carbonyl carbon. Carbon can't have five bonds so the pi electrons of the carbonyl double bond get pushed up onto the top oxygen, neutralizing its positive charge. We have now formed a tetrahedral intermediate. The carbon is bonded to its original alkyl chain, two distinct Oh groups, and the newly attached alcohol group. We have all the pieces connected, but we need to kick out the gouge. We need water to leave. But right now we just have Oh groups. In acidic conditions, Oh is a terrible leaving group. If it leaves, it forms a hydroxide ion, a strong base, which won't happen in an acid. So we need step three: Proton transfer. The molecule rearranges itself. A proton is plucked off the newly attached alcohol oxygen, which currently has a positive charge, and is transferred over to one of the original Oh groups. This turns the Oh group into an OH2 plus group. That is, essentially a fully formed molecule of water attached to the carbon, primed and desperate to leave. The couch is sitting by the door. Step four: Elimination The lone pair of electrons from the other oxygen atom, the one that used to be the carbonyl oxygen, collapses back down to reform the double bond. Carbon can't have five bonds, so it forcefully ejects the OH2 plus group. The water molecule breaks off and leaves. A final deprotonation restores the catalyst and the condensation is complete. You have synthesized your ester. Activation, nucleophilic attack, proton transfer, leaving group elimination. That logic making the target attractive, attacking it, preparing the leaving group, and kicking it out applies to almost every mechanism in organic chemistry. Okay, what is the next derivative child? Amides. In an amide, the hydroxyl group of the carboxylic acid is replaced by an amino group, meaning it contains a nitrogen atom. For naming, we just change the suffix of the parent chain to amimide. Propanamide, Potanamide. But nitrogen can form three bonds. What if the nitrogen atom has its own alkyl chains attached to it instead of just hydrogens? How do we name those branches so we know they're on the nitrogen and not the parent carbon chain? We use a specific locant. To specify that a substituent is located directly on the nitrogen atom, we use a capital N as a prefix. That capital N literally acts like a number. So if I have an ethyl group on the nitrogen of a three carbon chain, it is N ethylpropamide. And just like esters can form cyclic lactones, adenylase can form rings called lactams. Penicillin is the most famous example. Its activity relies entirely on the strain of a four membered beta lactam ring. Okay, the final derivative family, anhydrides. The word anhydride translates literally from Greek as without water. An anhydride is formed when two carboxylic acid molecules encounter each other and condense together. They join, a single molecule of water is expelled and you are left with two carbonyl groups bridged by a single central oxygen atom. Naming them is straightforward. You just take the name of the parent acid and replace the word acid with anhydride. Two acetic acid molecules condense to make acetic anhydride. But what if it's asymmetrical? What if I condense ethanoic acid and propanoic acid? You simply list the names of both parent acids alphabetically, followed by the word anhydride. Ethanoic, propanoic, anhydride. So we have the parent carboxylic acid and its three derivative children: esters, amides, and anhydrides. But here is the ultimate conceptual trap for this section. The MCAT will show you a pathway and ask if it proceeds spontaneously. Do these derivatives all react equally? Can I just swap an imide into an ester easily? Absolutely not. They are not interchangeable. There is a strict undeniably rigid reactivity hierarchy for nucleophilic acyl substitution. You must memorize this rule. Anhydrides are the most reactive. Esters are in the middle. Amides are the least reactive. Anhydrides esters amides. Let's explain why that hierarchy exists. Why are anhydrides so hot to react while amides are so incredibly stable? It comes down to a combination of induction, resonance, and leaving group stability. Let's look at an anhydride. It is three highly electronegative oxygen atoms closely packed together. All three are aggressively pulling electron density away from the two carbonyl carbons. That massive inductive effect makes those carbons incredibly electrophilic. They are desperate for electrons. So they are primed for nucleophilic attack. Right. And what happens when they are attacked? The central bond breaks and the leaving group is expelled. For an anhydride, the leaving group is a completely intact carboxylate anion. As we established earlier, a carboxylate anion is wonderfully resonant stabilized. It is a fantastic leaving group, so anhydrides have highly attractive targets and excellent escape routes. They want to react. Now let's look at the bottom of the ladder. Amides. Why are they so unreactive? Nitrogen is significantly less electron negative than oxygen. In fact, in an amide, the nitrogen atom's lone pair is actually electron donating through resonance. It pushes electron density toward the carbonyl carbon. It's sharing its wealth, which stabilizes the carbonyl carbon and makes it much less electrophilic. It isn't desperate for attack. Exactly. And furthermore, if a nucleophile were to somehow force an attack, the leaving group would have to be an amide ion and NH2 minuteus group. A localized negative charge on a nitrogen is highly basic and highly unstable. It is a terrible leaving group. So amides have an unattractive target and a terrible escape route. They resist reacting. They're totally content as they are. This is why nature uses amide bonds, peptide bonds, to hold our proteins together. We don't want our proteins reacting and falling apart in water. The practical takeaway for the listener answering a passage question is this: You can flow downhill, but you cannot flow uphill. Right, you can easily take a highly energetic reactive anhydride and mix it with an alcohol to form a stable ester. You're moving downhill to a lower energy state, but you cannot take a stable amide and react it to form an anhydride without pumping in a massive amount of external energy. Water flows downhill. Anhydride to ester to amide. That hierarchy solves a remarkable number of conceptual questions. And that brings us to the conclusion of our main material. Let's start with the ultimate functional group's priority ladder. When you are staring at a massive overwhelming molecule with six different functional groups, how do you determine who is the boss for naming? Oxidation state dictates priority. The summit is the carboxylic acid. Moving down, we hit its derivatives in order: anhydride, then ester, then amide. After the acid family, we drop to level two, terminal aldehydes, followed by internal ketones, then down to level one, alcohols, then down to level zero, alkenes, alkenes, and finally alkenes at the absolute bottom. Carboxylic acid and hydride ester, amide, aldehyde, ketone, alcohol, alkane, alkane. Next, let's recap reagents and reactions. How do we physically move up and down this ladder in a synthesis passage? The vertical flow up and down the ladder is entirely governed by oxidation and reduction. If you start with a primary alcohol, an Oh at the end of a chain, and apply a strong oxidizing agent like chromium, you synthesize an aldehyde. If you keep oxidizing, you drive it all the way up to a carboxyl acid. And if you want to move laterally into the derivatives, you don't use redox. You use condensation reactions. Condensing a carboxylic acid with an alcohol under acidic conditions yields an ester. That was our fissure esterification. Which ties directly into the core mechanisms you must recognize. We highlighted a fundamental difference in reactivity between level two and level three functional groups. Right, aldehydes and ketones undergo nucleophilic addition. Carboxylic acids and derivatives undergo nucleophilic acylase substitution. Let's clarify why. Aldehydes and ketones lack a good leaving group. You cannot easily kick off a hydrogen atom or an alkyl carbon chain. So when a nucleophile attacks the carbonyl carbon, the pi bond breaks, the electrons go to the oxygen, and the molecule simply absorbs the attacker. It adds the nucleophile onto its structure. Addition. But carboxylic acids and their derivatives esters, amides, anhydrides, they do possess viable leaving groups. They have an HOH and eight OR, an amino group. Because of that leaving group, when the nucleophile attacks and the tetrahedral intermediate forms, the carbonyl double bond has a pathway to reform. It crashes back down and forcefully kicks the leaving group out. The incoming nucleophile substitutes for the departing leaving group substitution. It is a massive conceptual distinction. Addition versus substitution Next in our recap: Stereochemistry. We spent time on the steering wheel for RS and on the same side for EZ. The high yield takeaway isn't just knowing how to assign them, but knowing formatting. Always assign RS and EZ first in your scratch work. Place them inside parentheses, separated by a comma if there are multiple chiral centers, and stick them at the very front of the completely alphabetized IUPIC name. The spatial configuration is the preface to the entire molecule. Let's touch on spectroscopy and lab clues. The MCAT loves giving you a physical visual observation in a passage and expecting you to deduce the starting functional group. What is the classic test for differentiating an aldehyde from a ketone? The Tollens test. It brilliantly exploits the structural difference we discussed earlier. Aldehydes have a terminal hydrogen. Because of that hydrogen, they can be oxidized one step further into carboxylic acids. Ketones, however, are sandwiched between carbons. They cannot be oxidized any further without violently breaking carbon bonds. The caboose can be modified, but the passenger car is locked in place. Precisely. Tollen's reagent contains a silver complex, specifically ag plus womb. When you mix it with an aldehyde, an oxidation reduction reaction occurs. The aldehyde is oxidized to a carboxylic acid, and the silver ion is reduced to pure, solid, metallic silver. This solid silver literally coats the inside of the glass reaction flask, creating a beautiful, physical mirror. So if the passage describes a clear solution suddenly coating the flask with a silver mirror, you know with 100% certainty that the starting material contained an aldehyde. If the flask stays clear, the molecule was likely a ketone, a highly visual lab clue. Finally, let's codify the top question traps that test takers consistently fall for. Trap number one, failing to find the longest carbon chain. The test makers will rarely draw the parent chain in a perfectly straight horizontal line. It will zigzag, double back on itself, or wrap around a ring. You must physically trace every possible path with your pencil to ensure you truly have the longest chain containing the highest priority functional group. Trap number two: the alphabetization prefix trap. Do not include quantity prefixes like di, tri, or hyphenated prefixes like sec- or tert when alphabetizing substituents. But you must include iso-, neo-, and cyclo. Misalphabetizing is the easiest way to pick a distractor answer choice. Trap three: Boiling points of carbonyls Assuming aldehydes and ketones have high boiling points comparable to alcohols, they do not. They have a hydrogen bond acceptor, but they entirely lack a hydrogen bond donor. They cannot hydrogen bond with themselves in a pure sample. Their boiling points are lower than alcohols, but higher than simple alkanes due to dipole interactions. This is a deep conceptual trap. Formal charge and oxidation numbers are two completely different, entirely artificial ways of modeling the physical reality of where electrons are. Formal charge assumes perfect, utopian sharing of covalent electrons. It cuts every bond right down the middle, ignoring electronegativity completely. Utopian equality. It pretends everyone shares perfectly. But oxidation numbers represent extreme, winner takes all capitalism. The oxidation model pretends every single bond is 100% ionic. It assumes all bonding electrons are completely surrendered to the more electronegative atom in the bond. The oxygen takes everything from the carbon. Neither model is exactly physically true in reality. Electron density exists on a probability spectrum, a cloud. But we use oxidation numbers, this extreme capitalistic model, to determine our nomenclature priorities and to track redox flow. Understanding the difference between those two accounting methods shows true mastery of the underlying physics. And that level of mastery is what separates a good score from a great score. Which brings us to the outro of our lecture. We have broken down the grammar, built the vocabulary, visualized the three d models, and predicted the reactions. But I want to leave you, the listener, with a final thought to mull over as you continue your studying tonight. Think about everything we just discussed over this hour. IUPEC nomenclature is entirely, 100%, a human invention. It is a bookkeeping fiction created by a committee of scientists in a room over a century ago. Electrons do not know their IUPAC name. A carbon atom doesn't know or care if we have arbitrarily labeled it R or S. It doesn't know it's the parent chain. The molecule just exists. It vibrates, it rotates, it follows the cold hard laws of quantum mechanics. It doesn't read textbooks. That is undeniably true. The map is not the territory. Right. The map is not the territory. Yet, this entirely artificial man made construct of prefixes, commas, hyphens, and alphabetization perfectly flawlessly maps to physical reality. By applying these made up grammatical rules, a scientist in Popio can look at a text string generated by a scientist in London, build a three d model in their head, and know exactly how that molecule will behave. They can predict precisely how it will boil, how it will interact with light, or how it will bind to a highly specific receptor in the human brain to cure a disease. The fact that human logic, syntax, and a few arbitrary rules can so accurately capture and predict quantum behavior is one of the profound beauties of chemistry. A perfect map allows you to navigate the territory flawlessly. It really is beautiful when you step back and look at it. The grammar dictates the reality. The most obvious is just linking all six carbons in a continuous straight horizontal line. We call that N hexane. Right, with the N standing for normal or unbranched. I mean, if they are all just non polar alkanes, they don't have hydrogen bonding. Right, no hydrogen bonding. And they don't have significant dipole moments either. They only interact through London dispersion forces. Yeah, right. And this is a highly testable concept for you. You must understand how London dispersion forces operate. Let's break that down. Well, are transient induced dipole interactions because electrons are constantly moving around the molecule occasionally just by random chance more electrons end up on one side than the other. Which creates a temporary microscopic magnet. Exactly. And this temporary magnet induces a similar magnetic pull in the molecule right next to it. But because these forces are so weak, they rely entirely on surface area contact to have any meaningful cumulative effect, right? Yes, that is the key. The more surface area contact between two molecules, stronger the overall London dispersion forces will be, and the harder it will be to boil them apart. Okay, so let's compare our straight chain and hexane to our highly branched two Alpano2 dimethylbutane. Let's do it. The straight chain and hexane is our long Lego bridge. Because it is an elongated, relatively flat molecule, millions of nhexane molecules can stack neatly on top of each other. Like Pringles? Yes. Almost like Pringles potato chips nestled perfectly in a can, they lay flat against one another, creating a massive amount of surface area contact along their entire carbon backbone. And that massive contact area means the cumulative London dispersion forces are maximized. The molecules just grip each other tightly. So it takes a lot of heat to boil them. Exactly. To force the molecules apart and into the gas phase requires a substantial amount of thermal energy. Consequently, N Hexane has a relatively high boiling point for its molecular weight, around 69 degrees Celsius. But the highly branched isomer, the two O2 dimethylbutane, is completely different. Very different. By branching the carbons, we have pulled the molecule inward. It is no longer a flat bridge, it is our compact Lego tower. In fact, it structurally resembles a sphere. If you take a bucket full of crumpled paper balls and pour them out onto a table, they do not stack neatly. They only touch each other tangentially at tiny microscopic points on their spherical surfaces. So the surface area contact drops dramatically. And less surface area contact means weaker London dispersion forces. The molecules just can't get a good grip on one another. So what happens to the boiling point? Because the intermolecular forces are significantly weaker, it requires far less thermal energy to agitate them and break them apart. Therefore, highly branched alkanes will invariably have lower boiling points than their straight chain structural isomers. Wow, okay. It is not an isomer at all, it is just a molecule that has been drawn in a confusing posture. That concept of molecules bending and shifting their posture provides the perfect conceptual bridge. Right, which introduces a whole new layer of complexity known as conformational isomers. Conformers. Yes. Conformational isomers or conformers are fundamentally the exact same molecule. The atomic connectivity has not changed. The formula has not changed. So what is changing? The only difference between two conformers is their natural continuous rotation around a single sigma covalent bond. Let's really drill down into the physics of this single sigma bond for a second. We learned in general chemistry that a sigma bond is formed by the head to head overlap of atomic orbitals. Because the electron density is concentrated directly between the two nuclei in a perfect symmetrical cylinder, rotating one atom relative to the other does not break that orbital overlap. Exactly. The bond stays perfectly intact while the atoms spin around it, literally like a wheel spinning on an axle. Contrast that with a double bond. A double bond contains a pi bond, which consists of electron clouds situated above and below the plane of the atoms. Right, parallel clouds. So if you try to twist a double bond, you would physically snap those parallel pi orbitals apart. You break the bond. Therefore, double bonds are locked. They cannot rotate. But single sigma bonds are free to rotate endlessly, driven by just the ambient thermal energy of the room. However, this rotation is not entirely frictionless. As the carbon atoms spin along that axle, the other groups attached to those spinning carbons, like hydrogens or bulky methyl groups, they start to swing past one another. Like fan blades. Yes. And sometimes they get too close for comfort, creating different levels of internal strain. The MCAT demands that you understand this strain, and specifically that you can rank the relative stability and energy of these different rotational states. This specific repulsion between the electrons in parallel, eclipsing bonds, is called torsional strain. It is uncomfortable. So the molecule uses its thermal energy to keep rotating. Let's push it another 60 degrees. Yeah, this brings us to the geometry of rings and the massive complications of cyclic conformation. When you tie a molecule into a circle, you fundamentally change the rules of rotation. Cycle alkanes have severely restricted mobility. They cannot spin endlessly like a free chain. And that restriction introduces a profound structural problem known generally as ring strain. To understand ring strain, we have to revisit the ideal geometry of a carbon atom. Every carbon in these cyclooalkane rings is sp3 hybridized. Right, single bonds only. As we know from VSEPR theory, a sp3 hybridized atom desperately wants to arrange its four single bonds as far away from each other as possible. Which results in a perfect tetrahedral angle of exactly one and nine point five degrees. 109.5 degrees is the baseline of comfort for an sp3 carbon. Now let's force that carbon into the smallest possible ring. Cyclopropane, a three carbon ring. Okay, cyclopropane. Geometrically, a three sided ring forms an equilateral triangle. Exactly. And the internal angles of a perfect equilateral triangle are mathematically locked at 60 degrees. So you have a carbon atom whose orbital mechanics demand a 109.5 degree spread. Yeah. And you're physically clamping those bonds down, crushing them into a 60 degree angle. That severe deviation from the ideal angle is called angle strain. The electron orbitals forming the bonds in cyclopropane cannot overlap directly head to head. Because they're bent. Right, they are forced to overlap at an awkward angle, creating what are sometimes colloquially called banana bonds. Banana bonds, I love that. Yeah, and this angle strain makes cyclopropane incredibly unstable, highly reactive, and prone to ring opening reactions just to relieve the pressure. As we move up to larger rings, the situation improves slightly, right? Cyclobutane, a four membered ring, has internal angles around 90 degrees. Still bad but better. Cyclopentane, a five membered ring, gets very close to comfort with angles near 108 degrees. But the absolute king of the cyclooalkanes, the molecule that essentially has its own dedicated subsection on the MCAT, is cyclohexane, a six membered ring. Yes, cyclohexane. Now, geometrically, if you draw a perfect flat hexagon on a piece of paper, the internal angles are exactly 120 degrees. Which is a significant deviation from the ideal 109.5 degrees. It is. Furthermore, if cyclohexane were perfectly flat, all of the hydrogen atoms attached to adjacent carbons would be perfectly eclipsed. Oh right, because they're all lined up. So that would cause massive torsional strain. Exactly. So a flat cyclohexane would suffer from both angle strain and torsional strain. So how does the molecule survive in nature? Because cyclohexane is incredibly stable. It survives by refusing to be flat. The molecule cheats geometry by puckering into a three-dimensional shape. Puckering? Yeah, by bending out of a flat plane, it can alleviate both the angle strain and the torsional strain. The most stable, low energy puckered conformation is called the chair conformation. I love visualizing the chair conformation. If you look at the molecule from a slight side angle, it literally resembles a reclining pool lounge chair. It really does. You have six carbons. The far left carbon points upward, forming the headrest of the chair. Then the structural backbone slants down, forming the seat where you would sit. And the far right carbon points downward, forming the footrest. That specific alternating puckered shape is a marvel of structural engineering. Because by adopting that exact geometry, every single carbon atom in the ring achieves an internal bond angle of exactly 109.5 degrees. Wow, so angle strain is completely 100% eliminated. Completely. Furthermore, if you were to look down any of the carbon carbon bonds in the chair using a Newman projection, you would find that every single bond in the entire molecule is perfectly staggered. So torsional strain is completely eliminated too. Structurally flawless. It's a perfectly relaxed molecule. But the complexity for the MCAT doesn't lie in the empty chair, right? It lies in where we put the substituents. Exactly. Every carbon in that six membered ring has two remaining bonds available to attach to other atoms The geometry of the chair forces these attachments into highly specific, rigid orientations. These orientations are divided into two strictly defined categories: axial and equatorial. Yes. Let's define the axial positions first. Think of the Earth's axis. Axial bonds stick straight up or straight down, completely parallel to the invisible vertical axis running through the center of the chair. And they follow a strict alternating pattern around the ring: Up, down, up, down. Right, so if you look at the headrest carbon, which is physically pointing up, its axial bond will point straight up to the ceiling. The very next carbon in the ring, moving down into the seat, will have its axial bond pointing straight down to the floor. The next carbon's axial bond points up, and so on. And the other category is equatorial. Think of the Earth's equator. Equatorial bonds stick out around the perimeter of the ring molecule. Right. They also alternate up and down, but they are slanted outward, radiating away from the center. So the key relationship to remember is that every carbon has one axial and one equatorial bond, and they must point in opposite stereochemical directions. That's right. If a carbon has an axial bond pointing straight up, its corresponding equatorial bond on that exact same carbon must be slanted slightly downward. Okay, we have built the chair and attached our group. Here is where the system becomes incredibly dynamic. This chair is not a static sculpture frozen in time. Not at all. The molecule possesses ambient thermal energy, meaning it is constantly vibrating, twisting and contorting. The molecule essentially turns itself inside out. Inside out. Yeah, the carbon that was acting as the headrest pointing up physically folds downward to become the new footrest. Simultaneously, the carbon that was the footrest pointing down snaps upward to become the new headrest. But the molecule cannot teleport from one chair to the other. It has to physically bend and warp through space to get there. Exactly. As it flips, the molecule must briefly pass through several highly unstable intermediate conformations. Conformations. The most important of these is the half chair conformation. Which introduces massive angle and torsional strain all over again. Precisely. The half chair is the energetic peak, the transition state of the flipping process. The molecule rapidly pushes through that agonizing state to reach the relaxed geometry of the flipped chair on the other side. Wow. This gives us a massive shortcut for the exam.
