Wherever the smaller groups end up during that flip, they end up. The wrecking ball wins. The largest electron cloud dictates the thermodynamic minimum. Every time. Brilliant. All right, let's take a conceptual step back. We have mastered structural isomers where the connectivity changes. We have extensively rotated single bonds and flipped cyclic chairs dealing with molecules that simply twist their posture in space. We are now crossing a major threshold. We are leaving the realm of conformational isomers and entering the more rigid, permanent world of configurational isomers and chirality. This is a profound shift in how we view the architecture of molecules. Conformational isomers are the exact same molecule just moving around its single bonds. Configurational isomers, however, are distinct stereoisomers that can only interconvert by physically breaking covalent bonds and reforming them in a different spatial arrangement. You cannot simply twist or flip these molecules to make them identical their geometry is permanently locked. And the foundational concept, the very heart of this entire section, is the chiral center. What specifically makes a carbon atom chiral? A chiral center, sometimes called a stereocenter, is strictly defined as an Sp3 hybridized carbon atom that is covalently bonded to four entirely different, unique chemical substituents. Four completely unique groups. That definition immediately rules out a lot of atoms. For instance, an sp2 hybridized carbon involved in a double bond can never be a chiral center. Never. A double bond is counted as two connections to the exact same atom, so it automatically fails fails the the requirement requirement for four different groups. Furthermore, the geometric reality of an sp2 hybridized carbon is that it is trigonal planar. It is perfectly flat. Right, flat. Because it is flat, it inherently possesses an internal plane of symmetry. You can slice it perfectly down the middle. Any molecule or atom with an internal plane of symmetry is instantly disqualified from being chiral. Also acaryl. Because it is attached to two identical hydrogen atoms, it only has three unique groups attached to it. It fails the four different groups test. It must be four distinct chemical entities: a hydrogen, a methyl group, an ethyl group, and a bromine, for example. Here is where the concept gets really intuitive for me. The word chirality is derived from the Greek word for hand. Chirality is literally just the concept of handedness. Exactly. If I look at my left hand and my right hand, they share the exact same structural connectivity. I have a thumb attached to the side of a palm, an index finger next to it, and so on. They are composed of the exact same parts connected in the exact same order. Yeah, but despite having identical connectivity, they are fundamentally different objects in three-dimensional space. Exactly. I cannot superimpose them. If I lay my left hand flat on a table, palm down, and try to place my right hand perfectly over it, also palm down, they do not match. No, they don't. The thumb on my right hand sticks out to the left and the thumb on my left hand sticks out to the right. The fingers don't align. The only way I can make them align perfectly is if I place them palm to palm. Which proves that they are not identical objects. They are exact mirror images of each other. That is the perfect macroscale biological demonstration of the concept. An enanti tumor is the exact molecular equivalent of your hands. Enanti tumors are defined as a pair of molecules that are non superimposable mirror images of each other. Non superimposable mirror images. They have the exact same connectivity, but their three-dimensional spatial arrangement is a perfect reverse reflection. This leads to a fascinating physical paradox. Because enantiomers have the exact same atomic connectivity and the exact same bonds, they must have identical internal properties. They have the same London dispersion forces, the same dipole interactions, the same ability to hydrogen bond. That is a high yield foundational concept. Because their intermolecular forces are identical, a pair of pure enantiomers will share the exact same boiling point, the exact same melting point, the exact same density, and the exact same refractive index. Which means standard chemistry techniques are useless here. If I have a flask containing a mixture of the left handed enantiomer and the right handed enantiomer, I cannot separate them using symbol distillation because they will both boil at the exact same temperature to the decimal point. You cannot separate them using standard physical methods, which raises the question: if they are physically identical in almost every measurable way, how do chemists even know they are different? How do we empirically measure chirality in a laboratory? They must differ in some way. They differ physically in only one highly specific, highly strange way how they interact with chiral environments, and most importantly for the MCAT, how their asymmetrical electron clouds interact with plane polarized light. This phenomenon is known as optical activity. Let's unpack the physics of the polarimeter because this instrument shows up constantly in exam passages. How does it actually work? Standard light, like the light emitted from a light bulb or the sun, consists of electromagnetic waves oscillating in all possible planes perpendicular to the direction of travel. Right. It's chaotic, vibrating up and down, left and right, and diagonally all at once. A polarimeter utilizes a specialized polarizing filter essentially a microscopic picket fence to physically block all of those chaotic waves except for the ones oscillating in one single specific two dimensional plane. This refined beam is called plane polarized light. Okay, so we have a perfectly flat vertical beam of light traveling forward through the machine. Next, we shine that plane polarized light through a specialized glass sample tube containing a pure concentrated liquid solution of our chiral molecule. As the flat plane of light travels through the liquid, the electric field of the light photons interacts with the asymmetrical handed electron clouds of the chiral molecules. Because the electron clouds are unsymmetrical, they exert a twisting force on the light wave. A twisting force. As the light propagates through the tube, the entire plane of the light is physically rotated. The beam goes in perfectly vertical, but it comes out the other end tilted at an angle. Exactly. The polarimeter measures that exact angle of rotation. If the pure chiral sample rotates the plane of light clockwise meaning to the right as you look at the beam, we classify that specific enantiomer as This is indicated by a lowercase read or a plus sign. And if we clean out the tube and put in a pure sample of its exact mirror image, the other enantiomer. Because it is a perfect mirror image, its electron clouds twist the light in the exact opposite direction. The pure sample will rotate the light counterclockwise to the left. We classify this enantiomer as a lavortatory, indicated by a lowercase l or a minus sign. So the math is perfectly symmetrical. If the right handed plus enantiomer rotates the light exactly plus 40 degrees, its mirror image minus enantiomer will rotate the light exactly minus 40 degrees. The magnitude of the twist is identical, but the direction is perfectly reversed. Correct. The degree of rotation is a physical constant for that specific molecule. Here is a massive point of confusion I want to clear up for the listener. Can I look at a two dimensional drawing of a molecule on my scratch paper, figure out its structural shape, and definitively predict whether it will be dextrorotatory or levoratory? Absolutely not. And test makers will aggressively try to trick you into thinking you can. Good to know. The plus and minus designations are purely experimental, empirical measurements. They have zero direct correlation with the structural rules we use to name the geometry of molecules, like the RNS system or the DNL system. So you can't just guess it from the drawing. You cannot look at a complex drawing and calculate how its electron cloud will twist a photon. The only way to know if a molecule is plus or minus is to physically synthesize it, put it in a glass tube, put it in a polarimeter and turn the machine on. That is a phenomenal rule to remember. Plus and minus are lab data, not structural data. Okay, what happens if I take a beaker and I carefully mix exactly 50% of the right handed plus enantiomer and exactly 50% of the left handed minus enantiomer together into a single solution. You have created what is known as a racemic mixture. And the stereochemical outcome of this mixture is beautiful, perfectly balanced physics. Because your solution contains an equal number of molecules trying to rotate the light plus 40 degrees to the right, and an equal number of molecules trying to rotate it minus 40 degrees to the left, the twisting forces as cancel each the light travels through the tube. Wow. So every time the light gets bumped to the right, it immediately hits a mirrored molecule and gets bumped back to the left. The net rotation, when the light exits through the tube, is exactly zero degrees. Yes, a racemic mixture is completely 100% optically inactive. It will not rotate plane polarized light at all despite being composed entirely of chiral molecules. That concept of the racemic mixture brings up a fascinating application and perhaps the single most common conceptual trap on the MSCAT regarding chirality. It connects the rigid rules of organic chemistry directly to the elegant machinery of human biology. You are anticipating a discussion on amino acid synthesis. I am. Let's talk about the Strecker synthesis and the Gabriel synthesis. We learned very early in biochemistry that human biology is incredibly stubbornly specific. It is. The ribosomes in our cells that build our proteins exclusively utilize left handed L amino acids. They are perfectly stereo specific molecular machines. If you give a ribosome a D amino acid, it rejects it entirely. Biology has evolved to be highly curated. But when an organic chemist tries to build an amino acid from scratch in a glass beaker in a laboratory using either the Strecker or Gabriel synthesis, they run into a massive, unavoidable statistical wall. Wall. Why is that? Both of these laboratory reactions rely on a nucleophile attacking a critical intermediate molecule to form the new chiral center of the amino acid. In the Strecker synthesis, the target intermediate is an imine. In the Gabriel synthesis, it involves an enolate type intermediate. And what do an imine and an enolate have in common, architecturally? They both contain Sofp2 hybridized carbons at the site of attack. As we established earlier, an sp2 hybridized carbon has a trigonal planar geometry. They are entirely flat two dimensional molecules. So imagine a completely flat microscopic piece of paper floating in the chaos of a beaker. When the nucleophile comes rushing in to attack that flat intermediate and form the new bond that will create the chiral center, it can approach from two directions: it can crash into the top face of the flat plane, or it can crash into the bottom face of the flat plane. And because the target is completely flat and perfectly symmetrical, there is absolutely no steric hindrance favoring one side over the other. The statistical probability of attacking the top versus attacking the bottom is exactly fiftyfifty. It is entirely random chance. Half the time, millions of nucleophiles attack from the top face, creating the right handed enantiomer. The other half of the time, millions of nucleophiles attack from the bottom face, creating the left handed enantiomer. So what's the result? The immutable statistical result is that these laboratory syntheses will always, without fail, yield an optically inactive, perfectly fiftyfifty racemic mixture of amino acids. Test makers absolutely love to describe a complex multi step Strecker or Gabriel synthesis in a passage and then simply ask in the questions, will the final product of this reaction rotate plane polarized light? Yes they do. If you didn't understand the planar intermediate, you might guess yes. But the answer is always no. Biology uses enzymes to force the nucleophile to attack from only one specific side, creating pure molecules. Undirected Beaker chemistry of planar targets relies on random collision, which always creates racemic mixtures. It highlights the profound difference between the random thermodynamic chaos of a flask and the curated directed machinery of a biological enzyme. Okay, let's keep building our architectural vocabulary. We just spent a lot of time talking about perfect mirror images. Section five of the material introduces diastereomers, cis trans isomers and mesocompounds. The defining characteristic of these stereoisomers is that they are not mirror images of each other. If an enantiomers are identical twins with opposite handedness, diastereomers are more like biological they share a massive amount of genetic code, they have the exact same atomic connectivity, but they are absolutely not identical and they are definitely not mirror images. How does a molecule structurally become a sibling rather than a twin? It requires a molecule to possess multiple chiral centers. The overarching rule for stereoisomers is two to the n power, where n is the total number of chiral centers in the molecule. That equation gives you the maximum number of possible stereoisomers. If a complex molecule has two distinct chiral centers, it can have up to two squared or four different stereoisomers. Let's visualize a molecule with carbon A and carbon B both being chiral centers. To make an enantiomer to build the perfect identical twin mirror image, I have to mathematically invert the stereochemistry at both carbon A, A and D carbon B. Right. If the original was RR, the enantiomer must be SS. Every single point of chirality must reflect perfectly. But to create a diastereomer, you only invert the stereochemistry at some, but not all, of the chiral centers. You might invert the stereochemistry at carbon A, but leave carbon B exactly the same as it was in the original molecule. Exactly. So an RR molecule becomes an SR molecule. Because half of the molecule was changed and flipped, but the other half stayed exactly the same, the resulting molecule is no longer a perfect mirror reflection of the original. It is a completely new, lopsided three-dimensional shape. And that lopsided shape leads to the most important physical distinction for the MCAT. Because diastereomers have different internal spatial arrangements relative to each other, the physical distance between their atoms changes. Oh, that makes sense. The overall dipole moment of the molecule changes. The way the electron clouds interact internally changes. Therefore, unlike enantiomers, diastereomers possess entirely different physical properties. Because their internal geometry is different, their intermolecular forces are different? Exactly. A pair of diastereomers will have different boiling points, different melting points, different densities, and different solubilities in various solvents. Wait, I had a logical question about that. Yeah. We established earlier that if I have a racemic mixture of two enantiomers, I cannot separate them by boiling them because their boiling points are identical to the decimal. Yes. But pharmaceutical chemists need to isolate pure enantiomers all the time to make safe drugs, like in the case of thalidomide. How can a chemist ever actually separate a racemic mixture in a real laboratory? That is where the genius application of diastereomers comes in. Chemists use a technique called chiral resolution. Since you cannot physically separate the enantiomers directly, you use chemistry to temporarily transform them into diastereomers. How does that chemical transformation work? You take your flask containing the unseparable racemic mixture of R and S and antiromers. Then you introduce a chemical reaction. You react the entire mixture with a single highly purified chiral region. Okay, let's say you reacted with a purely R resolving agent. Right, let's trace the math. The R anatomy humor in your mixture reacts with the R region to form a new combined molecule that is R R. Meanwhile, the S enantrum in your miniature reacts with that same R region to form a combined molecule that is SAR. Now look at the two new products you have created in your flask. They share the same connectivity. They differ at the first chiral center, but they're identical at the second chiral center. What is their structural relationship? They are diastereomers. We just manufactured diastereomers. Exactly. You have successfully transformed your unseparable twin enemers into sibling diseremers. And because we know disceremers have different physical properties, they now have different boiling points and different solubility. So you can separate them easily? Yes. You can easily use standard techniques to separate them. You can boil one off via fractional distillation or crystallize one out of the solution. And once you have physically separated the RR product into one flask and the SR product into another flask, you just run a simple cleavage reaction to chop off the resolving agent. Precisely. You reverse the reaction, remove the chiral region, and you are left with the pure R enantiomer in flask A and the pure S enantiomer in flask B. That is incredibly elegant chemistry. You manipulate the stereochemical relationships to temporarily alter the physical properties of the molecules. It is fundamental to modern pharmaceutical manufacturing. Let's quickly define a few specific high yield subtypes of diastereomers that the MCAT loves to test. First, epimers and anemers, which form the bedrock of carbohydrate biochemistry. Sugars are massive molecules with tons of chiral centers. Glucose has four chiral centers just in its straight chain form. Because they have so many chiral centers, sugars have dozens of diastereomers. Alburas are a specific narrow subtype of diastereomers that differ in their absolute configuration at exactly one and only one specific chiral center out of the entire molecule. For example, D glucose and D galactose are massive molecules that are structurally identical at every single carbon atom except for carbon four. The hydroxyl group on carbon four points right in glucose and left in galactose. Therefore, they are specifically classified as C4 epimers. Animers are an even more hyper specific subtype of epimers. They apply exclusively to ring structures. When a straight chain sugar folds up and attacks itself to form a closed ring, the carbonyl carbon that was attacked becomes a brand new chiral center. Okay, the anomeric carbon. Yes. This specific carbon is called the anomeric carbon. And depending on how the ring closes, the new hydroxyl group on that anomeric carbon can point down or up. Right. If the new hydroxyl group points down specifically trans to the CH2OH tail, it forms the alpha anomer. If it points up cis to the tail, it forms the beta anomer. So alpha and beta glucose are anomers. They're identical everywhere except that single anomeric carbon created during cyclization. Okay, alpha and beta anomers. Got it. Now what about cis trans isomers? We talked about them with double bonds, but they don't always involve chiral centers, Correct. Cis trans isomers, which are also known as geometric isomers, are a specific type of diastereomer that arises strictly from restricted rotation. This restricted rotation is usually caused by a rigid double bond or by the locked geometry of a cyclooalkane ring. Because the bond cannot freely spin, the substituents attached to it are permanently locked into spatial positions relative to each other. It's a simple relative relationship. Cis means the two largest substituents are locked on the same side of the double bond or pointing to the same face of the ring. Trans means they are permanently locked on opposite sides. Yes, it is relative positioning. But we need to move on and discuss the most dangerous counterintuitive trap in this entire section: mesocompounds. Okay, let me set this trap up. The basic foundational rule we learned earlier is that if a molecule possesses a chiral center, the molecule as a whole is chiral and therefore it will be optically active and rotate plane polarized light. That is exactly the assumption that test makers want you to blindly make. They will present a complex molecule in a passage, something like tartaric acid, which clearly and undeniably possesses two valid SPE3 chiral centers. A rushing student will spot the chiral centers, mentally apply the two to the NN rule, and instantly conclude that the molecule must be chiral and optically active. But organic chemistry is full of exception. It's a massive exception. For a molecule as a whole, macro entity to be considered chiral and optically active, it must completely lack an internal plane of symmetry. So what structural anomaly happens in a mesocompound? In a mesocompound, you have multiple valid chiral centers scattered throughout the molecule. However, by sheer geometric coincidence, the molecule as a whole happens to possess a perfect internal plane of symmetry right down its center. Wait, so the top matches the bottom? Yes, the top half of the molecule is a perfect, flawless, atom for atom mirror reflection of the bottom half. If the top is a perfect mirror reflection of the bottom internally, what happens to the optical activity when we put it in the polarimeter? Let's trace the path of the plane polarized light. The light enters the tube and hits the top half of the mesomolecule. Let's say that top chiral center is configured as an R center. It interacts with the light and rotates the plane 20 degrees to the right. Okay, the light is twisted plus 20. But as the light continues its journey through the molecule, it hits the bottom half. Because the bottom half is a perfect structural mirror image at the top, the lots of stereochemistry dictate that its chiral center must be an S configuration. Ah, I see where this is going. Therefore, the bottom half interacts with the light and rotates it exactly 20 degrees back to the left. The molecule internally perfectly cancels out its own optical rotation. Exactly. A mesocompound is essentially a racemic mixture built into a single individual molecule. Because the internal mirror plane causes the optical twisting forces to cancel out perfectly to zero, a mesocompound is entirely optically inactive despite possessing actual chiral centers. That's wild. Furthermore, because it has that plane of symmetry, if you build a mirror image of a meso compound, you will find it is perfectly superimposable on the original. It does not have an enantiomer. It's just the exact same actual molecule drawn twice. The rule here is vital. If you see a molecule on the exam that has multiple chiral centers, always, always pause. Take five seconds and try to draw a line of symmetry through the middle of it. If the top matches the bottom, it's meso. It will save you highly preventable lost points. It absolutely will. Symmetry destroys chirality. Yeah. This brings us to section six: Relative and Absolute Configurations. We have talked extensively about chiral centers, double bonds, and shapes. But how do chemists definitively name these spatial arrangements so they can communicate them globally? Right, how do we lock down the absolute configuration of a molecule permanently in its name? To do this consistently, the international chemistry community utilizes a highly standardized set of rules called the Kahn Ingold Pre Log Priority Rules, universally referred to as the CIP rules. This is the mechanical system we use to assign E and Z designations to complex double bonds and R and S designations to chiral centers. The foundational rule, the absolute bedrock for everything in the CIP system, is that priority is assigned strictly based on atomic number. Atomic number. Not the atomic mass, not the physical size of the electron cloud, the number of protons in the nucleus. Correct. You look exclusively at the atoms that are directly immediately attached to the chiral center of the double bond. The heavier the atom, meaning the higher its atomic number on the periodic table, the higher its priority ranking. So an oxygen atom beats a nitrogen atom. Yes, oxygen is eight, nitrogen is seven. Nitrogen beats a carbon atom, which is six. And carbon always beats a hydrogen atom, which is one. That seems simple enough if all four atoms are different elements. But organic chemistry is mostly carbon. What if my chiral center is attached to a methyl group, which is a carbon, and an ethyl group, which is also a carbon? If the immediately attached atoms are identical elements, you have a tie. To break the tie, you literally have to walk outward along the molecular chain, one single bond at a time. You look at the atoms attached to the tie breaking carbon and you list them in descending order of atomic number. You compare the lists of the two competing groups until you find the very first point of difference. So comparing a methyl group to an ethyl group, the first carbons tie. From the methyl carbon, I walk out and see it is attached to hydrogen, hydrogen, hydrogen. From the ethyl carbon, I walk out and see it is attached to a further carbon and two hydrogens. So its list is carbon, hydrogen, hydrogen. Now you compare the highest priority atom on each list. The ethyl group has a carbon. The methyl group only has hydrogens. Carbon beats hydrogen. The tie is broken at the first point of difference. The ethyl group receives the higher priority ranking. Okay, let's apply the CIP ranking system to double bonds first. Easy nomenclature. We use this for polysubstituted double bonds, where simple cis and trans designations aren't specific enough because there are three or four different groups attached. Right. To assign E or Z, you take your complex double bond and you mentally chop it directly in half vertically, completely separating the left carbon of the alkene from the right carbon of the alkene. I have split the bond down the middle, two separate sides. Now focus your attention entirely on the left carbon. It has two chemical groups attached to it. Apply the CIP atomic number rules to those two groups to determine which one has the higher priority. Put a mental star next to the winner. Okay, I compared the top left group to the bottom left group, found the heavier one, and started. Now, ignore the left side entirely and shift your focus to the right carbon. It also has two groups attached. Apply the CIP rules again, comparing top right to bottom right to find the higher priority group. Put a mental star next to that winner. So now I have two starred high priority groups. One on the left side of the double bond and one on the right side. Exactly. Now zoom out and look at the entire double bond as a whole. Are your two starred high priority groups located on the same horizontal side of the double bond, both on top or both on bottom? Or are they located on opposite sides? One top, one bottom. If the two heavy starred groups are on the same side, what is the designation? If the two heavy groups are on the same side, the absolute configuration is assigned a Z. The letter 'zee' comes from the German word 'seesamen' which translates to 'together'. 'seem side'. It is a terrible pun but it is an incredibly effective phonetic mnemonic. I will never forget it. And if the heavy groups are on opposite sides If they're on opposite sides, the designation is 'e'. This comes from the German word entgegen meaning opposite. Zem side and opposite side. It is highly reliable under test pressure. Zeman opposite. Perfect. Now let's tackle RS nomenclature, which is used to define the absolute configuration of SPE3 Tyrol centers. This This is where the three d mental gymnastics really kick in for students. It does require visualization, but there is a strict mechanical process to follow. First, identify your chiral center. Second, apply the CIP priority rules to rank all four attached groups from priority one, the heaviest and highest, down to priority four, the lightest and lowest. Because organic chemistry is carbon based, priority four is almost overwhelmingly a small hydrogen atom, right? Very frequently, yes. The third step is the most critical spatial requirement. You must mentally orient the three d molecule so that the lowest priority group, group number four, is pointing away from you, receding deep into the page on a dashed line. I have a visualization for this that always works for me. I imagine I am sitting in the driver's seat of a car. Okay, I like that. The chiral carbon atom itself is the steering column right in front of me. Group number four, the tiny hydrogen, is the steering shaft pointing away from me deep into the dashboard. The other three groups, priority one, two, and three form the physical steering wheel popping out at me ready for my hands. That is an excellent spatial visualization. Because once group four is pointing away into the dashboard, you simply look at the steering wheel. You draw a curved arc connecting priority group one to priority group two to priority group three. If I draw the arc from one to two to three and my hand moves in a clockwise direction, I am effectively turning the steering wheel to the right. Clockwise is right. The absolute configuration is assigned as R. This originates from the Latin word rectus meaning right or straight. And if the arc from one to two to three goes in a counterclockwise direction, I am turning the steering wheel to the left. Counterclockwise is left. The configuration is assigned as S. This originates from the Latin word sinister, meaning left. Okay, that mechanical process seems straightforward enough provided Group four is neatly pointing into the dashboard. But the MCAT is not a kind exam. What is the most common, cruel trap they set regarding RNS? The test makers know that rotating three d objects in your working memory is cognitively taxing. To induce errors, they will intentionally draw the molecule backwards. They will put the lowest priority group, the hydrogen, on a solid wedge. Which means it is pointing straight out of the page, poking you directly in the eye. It is the exact opposite of where it geometrically needs to be to apply the steering wheel rule. If I try to mentally pick up that entire complex molecule and rotate it 180 degrees in three d space under severe time pressure, I am almost guaranteed to misplace a substituent and make a mistake. Which is exactly why you shouldn't even try to mentally rotate There is a foolproof mechanical fail safe for this exact trap. Oh! If group four is pointing at you on a wedge, completely ignore that fact for a moment. Just look at groups one, two and three exactly as they are drawn on the page. Draw your normal circle from one to two to three. Okay, let's say I draw the circle and the arc goes clockwise. That would normally be in R configuration. Exactly. Figure out what the configuration looks like it should be based on the drawing. But because you know the molecule is facing completely backward because group four is pointing the wrong way, you know your initial answer must also be backward. So you simply take your final answer and reverse it. Yes. If it looks clockwise or R, the true absolute configuration of the molecule is actually S. If it looks S, it's actually R. That is a brilliant shortcut. Just run the process normally, and if the hydrogen is wedged, flip the final letter. No complex mental rotation required. It saves precious time and prevents massive unforced errors on test day. This is a perfect transition into our final major topic, section seven. Fisher Projections and Dynamic Tautomerization. We have spent the last hour talking about drawing wedges and dashes to represent three d space, but biochemists use a completely different, highly stylized system for drawing massive molecules like sugars and amino acids. Yes, they rely heavily on the Fischer Projection. A Fischer Projection is a brilliant, highly condensed shorthand for representing a complex, multi centered three d molecule on a flat, two d piece of paper. It is heavily utilized in biochemistry to draw monosaccharides like d glucose. Glucose has four contiguous chiral centers stacked on top of each other. If you tried to draw all of those with standard wedges and dashes, the drawing would become an incomprehensible, messy thicket of lines. So instead of wedges and dashes, they draw a simple vertical line crossed by multiple horizontal lines. Structurally, it looks like a tall ladder or perhaps a vertical spine with horizontal ribs sticking out. But those simple straight lines carry very strict, very specific three d geometric information. They do. In a fissure projection, every single point where a horizontal line intersects the vertical spine represents a carbon atom and usually it represents a chiral center. And the absolute defining rule of the fissure projection is this: All horizontal lines represent covalent bonds that are physically projecting out of the page toward you on invisible wedges. All vertical lines represent bonds that are physically receding into the page away from you on invisible dashes. Curving away backward into the page. The hug analogy is perfect. The horizontal arms reach out. Because this drawing convention is so incredibly strict, the MCAT loves to test your ability to mentally manipulate these fissure projections on scratch paper without accidentally drawing a completely different molecule. Right. They will frequently ask, which of the following graphical operations retains the original stereochemistry of the molecule? So what are the valid legal moves I can make on a piece of paper to manipulate a fissure projection? There are two strictly valid moves that guarantee the exact same stereochemistry is retained. First, you can take the entire piece of paper and rotate it by exactly 180 degrees. Okay, flipping it upside down. Because you're flipping the drawing entirely upside down, the horizontal arms that were coming out at you on the top are still horizontal arms coming out at you on the bottom. The stereochemistry is perfectly preserved. Rotating 180 degrees is a safe move. What is the second trick? The second is a specific swapping trick. If you hold one chiral center steady and you swap two pairs of substituents attached to it simultaneously, the stereochemistry is retained. Swapping two pairs essentially performs a mathematical double negation, mathematically bringing you right back to where you started. And what are the invalid moves? The traps that will cause me to inadvertently draw the mirror image enantiomer and get the question wrong. The most dangerous invalid move is rotating the piece of paper by 90 degrees. Think back to your hug analogy. If you rotate the drawing 90 degrees, the horizontal arms that were reaching out to hug you have now become vertical lines. And the rule states that vertical lines must recede away from you. By rotating the drawing 90 degrees, you have fundamentally inverted the three d geometry of every single bond on that carbon. You just turned wedges into dashes. You just mutated an R chiral center into an S chiral center? Exactly. The other invalid move is swapping just one single pair of substituents on a chiral center. If you simply swap the group on the left with the group on the right, you have manually created the mirror image, you have inverted the stereocenter to its enantiomer. Okay, those are the mechanical graphical rules of the drawing. Let's talk about how this drawing is actively applied to relative configuration in biochemistry, specifically the DNL system for naming sugars. The DNL system is an older relative naming convention. Unlike R and S, which are absolute, DNL compare the stereochemistry of a massive sugar to a tiny standard molecule glyceraldehyde. This system is extremely high yield for the biology sections of the MCAT. To assign a D or L designation to a complex fissure projection of a large sugar, you must look at one specific carbon. The highest number to chiral carbon, which is the chiral center that is furthest away from the most oxidized carbonyl group. Let's visualize a standard drawing of glucose. The aldehyde carbonyl group is drawn at the very top. That is carbon one. So we look all the way down the vertical spine of the molecule to the bottommost cross intersection. That is the highest numbered chiral carbon, carbon five. Exactly. You focus your analysis entirely on that single specific carbon five intersection. If the hydroxyl group, the Oh on that bottom chiral carbon is pointing to the right side of the Fisher projection, it is classified as a D sugar. If the hydroxyl group is pointing to the left side, it is classified as an L sugar. Right is D, left is L. And this structural designation is biologically vital because human metabolism is stereo specific. When you eat breakfast, the enzymes in your body almost exclusively recognize and metabolize D sugars just like your ribosomes utilize to amino build muscle. The biological specificity is profound. D sugars fuel life on Earth. Okay, want to use our remaining time to push back on something we have been building for the last hour. Oh, let's hear it. We have meticulously built all these incredibly rigid rules for absolute configuration. We assign RNS, we map out rigid fissure projections, we talk about enantiomers as permanent locked architectural structures. But in the reality of an organic chemistry flask, molecules are not static drawings. They vibrate, they move, they react with acids and bases. Can a molecule ever just spontaneously lose its permanent crality on its own? That is an incredibly perceptive question that strikes at the heart of organic reactivity. And the answer leads us to our final and perhaps most complex conceptual trap: dynamic tautomerization and the phenomenon of alpha racemization. Let's define tautomerization clearly. We're specifically talking about the keto enol dynamic equilibrium. Yes. If you have a ketone in solution, which is a carbon atom double bonded to an oxygen atom, you must always examine the adjacent carbon, known as the alpha carbon. If that alpha carbon has at least one hydrogen atom attached to it, the entire molecule is capable of undergoing tautomerization. How does that happen mechanically? A base in the solvent can pull off that slightly acidic alpha proton. When it does, the electrons swing down to form a new carbon double bond, and the pi electrons of the original carbonyl group swing up onto the oxygen, which then grabs a proton from the solvent to become an alcohol. The molecule essentially undergoes a structural flip from being a ketone to being an alkene with an alcohol attached And these two forms constantly flip back and forth in a dynamic equilibrium. They do. Now here is the severe stereochemical consequence of that movement. Let's assume your starting ketone had a chiral alpha carbon. It was attached to four different groups, maybe a methyl group, an ethyl group, the massive carbonyl group, and the single alpha hydrogen. Because it has four different groups, it is a rigid, sp3 hybridized tetrahedral chiral center. It possesses a very specific, permanent RRS handedness. But when that ketone tautomerizes into an enol When it tautomerizes into the enol form, a new carbon carbon double bond is formed directly at that exact alpha position. What is the hybridization of a double bonded carbon? It is csp2 hybridized. And what is the inescapable geometry of an sp2 carbon? It is trigonal planar. It is completely Exactly. The very moment the molecule flips into the enol form, that originally tetrahedral alpha carbon is forcefully flattened out into a two d plane. It entirely loses its tetrahedral shape. Because it is flat, it instantly gains an internal plane of symmetry. By flattening out, it literally erases its own chirality. The handedness, the stereo memory of the molecule is completely gone. It is entirely eradicated. The enol intermediate is ocryl. Now, tautomerization is a dynamic reversible equilibrium. The molecule will not stay in enol forever. It will eventually flip back into the keto form. Right. The carbon carbon double bond breaks and the flat alpha carbon has to reach out and grab a proton from the solvent to rebuild its P3 tetrahedral geometry. But because it is starting the rebuilding process from a completely flat planar enol, it encounters the exact same statistical problem as the Strecker synthesis we discussed earlier. It is the exact same brutal statistical reality. When the flat alpha carbon reaches for a proton, it can grab one by attacking from the top face of the flat enol plane, or it can grab one by attacking from the bottom face of the plane with an equal unguided fiftyfifty probability. So if it happens to grab a proton from the top face, it might rebuild the original R enantiomer. But if it grabs a proton from the bottom face, it accidentally builds the opposite S enantiomer. Yes. This spontaneous dynamic process is called alpha racemization. Even if you start an experiment with a flask full of 100% pure, highly optically active R ketone, if you leave it in an aqueous solution, the molecules will continuously tautomerize back and forth, one by one. Slowly, molecule by molecule, they flatten out, erase their chirality, and rebuild randomly. Until eventually, over time, the entire flask equilibrates into a perfect fiftyfifty racemic mixture of R and S enantiomers out of nowhere and the optical activity of your flies drops to zero. It is the ultimate stereochemical trap on the MCAT. You cannot assume a chiral center is permanent and locked if it sits adjacent to a carbonyl group capable of tautomerization. The inherent chemical reactivity itself will eventually erase the carefully built architecture. That is a phenomenal connection to end on. It seamlessly ties everything together from hybridization and planar geometry to statistical probability to the physical measurement of optical activity. It really does circle back. Our cellular metabolism runs almost exclusively on right handed D sugars. But as we learned from a purely chemical and statistical perspective, an undirected laboratory beaker always generates fiftyfifty racemic mixtures. Why did all of Earth's biology become permanently locked into a left handed amino acid architecture when the mirror image was equally available? The entire orbital contains negatively charged electrons. The plus and minus signs refer strictly to the mathematical sign of the wave function. Right, because electrons act like waves, so it's like a wave in a pool. The crest is above the zero line positive phase, the trough is below negative phase. Precisely. And when two atomic orbitals approach each other, their wave functions interact. They mathematically combine to create entirely new, shared molecular orbitals. Okay, let's visualize this. If I have a wave with a positive crest, and you have a wave with a positive crest, and they crash into each other. Those mathematical signs add together. That is constructive interference. So they build a super wave. Exactly. The wave functions reinforce each other and the amplitude drastically increases. And what does that actually mean for the molecule? Well, in physical chemistry, that increased amplitude translates to a massive accumulation of electron density directly between the two atomic nuclei. Oh, so that dense cloud of negative electrons sits right between the positive nuclei and acts like glue. Yes, it pulls the nuclei together and shields them from repelling each other. We call that a bonding molecular orbital. Which is really stable, right. Highly stable, very low energy. But the math can go the other way. What if your positive crest collides with my negative trough? They cancel out. A plus one and a minus one equals zero, the wave just goes completely flat. That is destructive interference. Instead of accumulating electron density between the nuclei, the wavefunctions annihilate each other. Leaving a node right in the middle. Right, and with absolutely zero electron density between them, those two positively charged nuclei are totally exposed to each other. So they just violently repel. Exactly. That creates an antibonding molecular orbital. It's a super high energy, totally unstable state. So a molecule obviously wants its electrons in the bonding orbitals pulling things together. Of course. Now let's look at the physical shape of that constructive overlap because that dictates the flexibility of the whole molecule. When two atomic orbitals point directly at each other and overlap head to head, they form a sigma bond. I picture a sigma bond like a cylinder connecting the two atoms. Right, the electron density is concentrated entirely along that linear axis between the nuclei. And because that cylinder is completely symmetrical around the axis, the atoms can just spin and rotate freely. Right. Freely without ever interrupting the orbital overlap. Like spinning a wheel on an axle. The axle doesn't care if the wheel turns. That free rotation is the absolute hallmark of a single bond. But, molecules aren't just single bonds. Right, when we add a double or triple bond, we lose that flexibility because we add a pi bond. And a pi bond is formed completely differently. Instead of pointing directly at each other, two unhybridized p orbitals align perfectly parallel to each other. They stand up vertically, side by side. Yes. And because they are parallel, they can't overlap directly between the nuclei. Because the sigma bond is already sitting in that space. Correct. So the electron clouds of the p orbitals bleed into each other above and below the plane of the molecule. It's a side to side overlap. And this is where it physically locks in. Because you have density above and below the axis, you physically cannot twist those atoms anymore. If you try to rotate one of the carbons in a double bond, you would literally have to physically rip that side to side orbital overlap apart. Which would take a ton of energy. An enormous energy barrier, yes. Pi bonds lock a molecule into a rigid planar shape. And what's fascinating here is this isn't just trivia, this is the structural foundation of biology. Yeah, like if carbon double bonds could spin freely, the lipid bilayers protecting your cells would just melt into liquid mush. Exactly. Or consider your vision.
